Abstract 
For the first time in the world, we succeeded in synthesizing the room-temperature superconductor 
(Tc â¥ 400 K, 127 oC) working at ambient pressure with a modified lead-apatite (LK-99) structure. 
The superconductivity of LK-99 is proved with the Critical temperature (Tc), Zero-resistivity, 
Critical current (Ic), Critical magnetic field (Hc), and the Meissner effect. The superconductivity 
of LK-99 originates from minute structural distortion by a slight volume shrinkage (0.48 %), not 
by external factors such as temperature and pressure. The shrinkage is caused by Cu2+ substitution 
of Pb2+(2) ions in the insulating network of Pb(2)-phosphate and it generates the stress. It 
concurrently transfers to Pb(1) of the cylindrical column resulting in distortion of the cylindrical 
column interface, which creates superconducting quantum wells (SQWs) in the interface. The heat 
capacity results indicated that the new model is suitable for explaining the superconductivity of 
LK-99. The unique structure of LK-99 that allows the minute distorted structure to be maintained 
in the interfaces is the most important factor that LK-99 maintains and exhibits superconductivity 
at room temperatures and ambient pressure. 
*stsaram@qcentre.co.kr 
â ywkwon@korea.ac.kr 2 
Introduction 
Since the discovery of the first superconductor(1), many efforts to search for new room- 
temperature superconductors have been carried out worldwide(2, 3) through their experimental 
clarity or/and theoretical perspectives(4-8). The recent success of developing room-temperature 
superconductors with hydrogen sulfide(9) and yttrium super-hydride(10) has great attention 
worldwide, which is expected by strong electron-phonon coupling theory with high-frequency 
hydrogen phonon modes(11, 12). However, it is difficult to apply them to actual application 
devices in daily life because of the tremendously high pressure, and more efforts are being made 
to overcome the high-pressure problem(13). 
For the first time in the world, we report the success in synthesizing a room-temperature and 
ambient-pressure superconductor with a chemical approach to solve the temperature and pressure 
problem. We named the first room temperature and ambient pressure superconductor LK-99. The 
superconductivity of LK-99 proved with the Critical temperature (Tc), Zero-resistivity, Critical 
current (Ic), Critical magnetic field (Hc), and Meissner effect(14, 15). Several data were collected 
and analyzed in detail to figure out the puzzle of superconductivity of LK-99: X-ray diffraction 
(XRD), X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), Electron Paramagnetic Resonance Spectroscopy 
(EPR), Heat Capacity, and Superconducting quantum interference device (SQUID) data. 
Henceforth in this paper, we will report and discuss our new findings including superconducting 
quantum wells associated with the superconductivity of LK-99.ChatGPTThis paper by Sukbae Lee, Ji-Hoon Kim, and Young-Wan Kwon reports the successful synthesis of the world's first room-temperature superconductor that operates at ambient pressure. Named LK-99, it has a critical temperature (Tc) of over 400K (or 127Â°C).The superconductivity of LK-99, which has a modified lead-apatite structure, was proven through a range of measurements including critical temperature (Tc), zero-resistivity, critical current (Ic), critical magnetic field (Hc), and the Meissner effect. These properties are the hallmarks of superconductivity.This material achieves superconductivity not from external factors such as temperature or pressure but from a minute structural distortion caused by the substitution of Cu2+ ions for Pb2+(2) ions in the insulating network of Pb(2)-phosphate. This substitution causes a slight volume shrinkage (0.48%), which generates stress that distorts the interface of the cylindrical column. This distortion creates superconducting quantum wells (SQWs) at the interface.Heat capacity results indicated that this model effectively explains the superconductivity of LK-99. The unique structure of LK-99, which allows it to maintain these minute distortions, is crucial for its ability to exhibit superconductivity at room temperatures and ambient pressure.The researchers point out that previous attempts at developing room-temperature superconductors, such as those involving hydrogen sulfide and yttrium super-hydride, were not practical for everyday use due to the high pressures required. The successful development of LK-99 solves this problem, marking a significant advancement in the field of superconductor technology.superconductors, the large absorption EPR signal appeared at a very low temperature and below 
1000 Oe external magnetic field(53, 54), and we also observed with YBCO and Bi2212. The signal 
below 1000 Oe external magnetic field can be interpreted as a signal by superconducting 
electrons(53, 54). 
Josephson installed a thin insulator between superconductors and discovered tunneling through 
which current flows. Tunneling occurred between the superconductors(55). Likewise, if electrons 
are moved by tunneling between SQWs, the resistance will naturally be zero. Tunneling between 
SQWs is likely to be possible, as SQWs are expected to be formed at 3.7 Ã ~ 6.5 Ã intervals in 
LK-99. According to Kimâs paper(52), he used 1250 K Debye temperature for his calculation of 
room-temperature superconductor, which is mentioned for hydride in 1968 by Ashcroft(56). Even 
in that case, he argued that the electron-electron interaction contributes to the higher temperature 
superconductivity than the electron-phonon interaction. In LK-99 system, the applied current 
seems to be transported through the correlated SQWs of the cylindrical column via the tunneling 
process and coherently transported in 3-dimension with all SQWs together. The additional 
13 
experimental results and discussions on LK-99 will be published immediately in the next paper, 
including an interesting controllable levitation phenomenon and the coexistence of magnetism and 
superconductivity, theoretical calculation, etc. LK-99 will be an attractive substance for many 
researchers that can solve various confusing puzzles such as the pairing process and coexistence 
with magnetism, etc., related to superconductivity. 
Consequently, why does LK-99 exhibit superconductivity at room temperature and ambient 
pressure? This is because the stress generated by the Cu2+ replacement of Pb(2)2+ ion was not 
relieved due to the structural uniqueness of LK-99 and at the same time was appropriately 
transferred to the interface of the cylindrical column. In other words, the Pb(1) atoms in the 
cylindrical column interface of LK-99 occupy a structurally limited space. These atoms are entirely 
affected by the stress and strain generated by Cu2+ ions. Therefore, SQWs can be generated in the 
interface by an appropriate amount of distortion(57) at room temperature and ambient pressure 
without a relaxation. From this point of view, the stress due to volume contraction by temperature 
and pressure is relieved and disappeared in CuO- and Fe-based superconductor systems because 
the relaxation process cannot be limited due to the structural freedom. Therefore, they need an 
appropriate temperature or pressure to limit the structural freedom and to achieve the SQW 
generation. The LK-99 is a very useful material for the study of superconductivity puzzles at room 
temperature. All evidence and explanation lead that LK-99 is the first room-temperature and 
ambient-pressure superconductor. The LK-99 has many possibilities for various applications such 
as magnet, motor, cable, levitation train, power cable, qubit for a quantum computer, THz 
Antennas, etc. We believe that our new development will be a brand-new historical event that 
opens a new era for humankind.ChatGPTThe researchers discuss how superconducting electrons are responsible for the signal they observed at a very low temperature and below an external magnetic field of 1000 Oe. They draw an analogy with the concept of "tunneling" discovered by Josephson, in which a current can flow through a thin insulator installed between superconductors.The paper argues that a similar tunneling process might occur in LK-99 between Superconducting Quantum Wells (SQWs) that are expected to form at intervals of 3.7 Ã ~ 6.5 Ã. The current applied to the LK-99 system seems to be transported through these SQWs via the tunneling process and is coherently transported in three dimensions across all SQWs.This superconductivity is possible because the stress generated by the substitution of Cu2+ ions for Pb(2)2+ ions is not relieved due to LK-99's unique structure but is appropriately transferred to the interface of a cylindrical column. The Pb(1) atoms in this interface are entirely affected by the stress and strain generated by the Cu2+ ions, leading to the generation of SQWs in the interface by an appropriate amount of distortion at room temperature and ambient pressure without relaxation.The researchers note that the volume contraction due to temperature and pressure is not relieved in CuO- and Fe-based superconductor systems due to their structural freedom. Therefore, they require specific temperature or pressure to limit the structural freedom and generate SQWs.The paper concludes by stating that LK-99 is the first room-temperature and ambient-pressure superconductor. It has potential for various applications including magnets, motors, cables, levitation trains, power cables, qubits for quantum computers, THz Antennas, etc. The researchers believe their development will be a groundbreaking event opening a new era for humankind.Could you give a metaphor or analogy to explain the proposed mechanism?ChatGPTAbsolutely! Here's a simplified analogy that can help understand the concept. Imagine a crowded hallway in a school, with students (electrons) trying to move from one end to the other. Normally, the students bump into each other and the walls, causing resistance and slowing down their progress. This is similar to how electrons behave in normal conductive materials, where their movement is hampered by resistance, leading to energy loss in the form of heat.Now, let's introduce a superconductor into the scenario. When the school installs an advanced system of moving walkways (like the ones in airports) that are precisely coordinated to help students glide smoothly along the hallway, students can move quickly and efficiently from one end to the other without bumping into each other or the walls. This results in zero resistance, much like the zero electrical resistance in a superconductor.The distortion in LK-99's structure, due to the substitution of Cu2+ ions for Pb2+(2) ions, can be compared to installing these moving walkways. It creates 'superconducting quantum wells' or special zones where the electrons can move freely without resistance. The novel thing about LK-99 is that this system works at room temperature and ambient pressure, meaning we don't need to cool down the school (lower the temperature) or squeeze the hallways (apply pressure) to make the walkways work. This is a major breakthrough in superconductor research.Ok that is indeed significant if it is reproducible and can be done at an affordable cost.ChatGPTYes, you're absolutely right. The creation of a room-temperature, ambient-pressure superconductor like LK-99 could revolutionize many aspects of technology, from energy transmission to computing. Furthermore, the process for making this superconductor needs to be cost-effective on a large scale.Developing new materials, especially those with highly specialized properties like superconductors, often involves expensive processes and rare ingredients. The challenge is to make these processes more affordable and sustainable so that the benefits of such technologies can be enjoyed widely.Research in this area is ongoing, and if successful, we could witness a paradigm shift in various industries such as energy, transportation, and computing.Youâll notice that when electrical objects run, they often emit heat. Take, for example, your phone when youâve scrolled social media for too long â or your laptop trying to open Photoshop. As these objects work, some of their energy escapes as heat. This is considered wasted energy, and is thanks to resistance in the conductor.

The distinction between a conductor and superconductor is that a superconductor transmits electricity without this wasted energy â in other words, its material faces no resistance, and will never generate any heat as electricity passes through it.

Until July 2023, the superconductors that scientists had created could only work at extremely low temperatures. A room temperature superconductor has been a long-sought goal for physicists because it could revolutionise our energy industries â and, increasingly, because this technology could very well allow us to mitigate the effects of climate change.

Whatâs The Discovery?
On 22 July 2023, scientists from Korea University, Sukbae Lee, Ji-Hoon Kim and Young-Wan Kwon, published a pre-print paper detailing an exciting discovery: theyâve created the worldâs first room temperature superconductor using a material dubbed âLK-99â.

Why Is This Significant?
If proven through reproduction, the LK-99 room temperature superconductor could have seriously positive repercussions for energy and the development of future technologies.

Revolutionising The Energy Industry
Electricity transmitted over a superconductor would face little to no resistance, so could be transported over long distances very efficiently. This will impact sustainability because there will be less waste, and therefore less additional energy would need to be generated. In turn, this would lead to a decrease in greenhouse gas emissions from power generation.

This technology could also help us better integrate renewable energy into the national energy grid, as one current challenge faced is how far renewable sources are from population centres.

Should We Be Dubious?
While exciting â and a hopeful discovery â the paper that was submitted is pre-print, which means it has yet to go through peer review. Similar work has faced serious scrutiny, and been disproven at this stage. The work of one University of Rochester researcher, who claimed to discover a similar superconductor in 2021, saw his paper recently retracted upon the discovery of errors and data fabrication.

In addition, if the discovery cannot be repeated or scaled, its practical impact in the immediate will be minimal.

For decades, physicists have dreamed of discovering a material that could effortlessly convey electricity at everyday temperatures, a feat that would save gargantuan amounts of energy and revolutionize modern technology.

Writing in the journal Nature, a team of researchers announced on Wednesday that they have done just that. They have made a superconductor that works at 58 degrees Fahrenheit, the temperature of a cool autumn day.

This material is still far from practical, produced in only minute quantities and under immense pressures usually found closer to the Earthâs core. But the scientists hope that with further experimentation they can devise a variation of their material that remains a superconductor even after that pressure is removed.

âUltimately, we want to bring the pressure to almost ambient pressure, to actually have an actual application,â said Ranga P. Dias, a professor of physics and mechanical engineering at the University of Rochester and the senior author of the Nature paper.

It is really surprising that there would be reporting on an article in the pre-print stage, does that really mean before peer review?ChatGPTYes, you're correct. When an article is in the "pre-print" stage, it means it has not yet undergone the peer-review process. Peer review is a crucial part of scientific research, as it involves independent experts evaluating the research for validity, significance, and originality.Reporting on pre-print articles can be somewhat controversial. On one hand, it allows for rapid dissemination of potentially significant findings, which can be particularly valuable in fast-moving fields or situations like a public health crisis. On the other hand, without the quality control offered by peer review, there's a higher risk of spreading inaccurate or misleading information.This is why such reports often include disclaimers, noting that the research is preliminary and has not yet been peer-reviewed. As such, while the reported creation of LK-99 as a room-temperature superconductor is a potentially exciting development, it's important to approach the findings with a degree of caution until they have been thoroughly vetted by the scientific community.NEWS

Ranga Dias will have a second paper revoked. A journalâs investigation found apparent data fabrication.

Ranga Dias, a physicist at the University of Rochester in New York, is at the centre of a controversy over room-temperature superconductivity claims.Credit: Lauren Petracca/New York Times/Redux/eyevine

A prominent journal has decided to retract a paper by Ranga Dias, a physicist at the University of Rochester in New York who has made controversial claims about discovering room-temperature superconductors â materials that would not require any cooling to conduct electricity with zero resistance. The forthcoming retraction, of a paper published by Physical Review Letters (PRL) in 20211, is significant because the Nature news team has learnt that it is the result of an investigation that found apparent data fabrication.

PRLâs decision follows allegations that Dias plagiarized substantial portions of his PhD thesis and a separate retraction of one of Diasâs papers on room-temperature superconductivity by Nature last September. (Natureâs news team is independent of its journals team.)


Stunning room-temperature-superconductor claim is retracted

After receiving an e-mail last year expressing concern about possible data fabrication in Diasâs PRL paper â a study, not about room-temperature superconductivity, but about the electrical properties of manganese disulfide (MnS2) â the journal commissioned an investigation by four independent referees. Natureâs news team has obtained documents about the investigation, including e-mails and three reports of its outcome, from sources who have asked to remain anonymous. âThe findings back up the allegations of data fabrication/falsification convincingly,â PRLâs editors wrote in an e-mail obtained by Nature. Jessica Thomas, an executive editor at the American Physical Society, which publishes PRL, declined to comment.ChatGPTThe news you've provided indicates that the research work by physicist Ranga Dias, which has made claims about room-temperature superconductivity, is under serious scrutiny. A forthcoming retraction of one of his papers published by Physical Review Letters (PRL) suggests data fabrication, which severely undermines the credibility of his findings. This retraction follows a separate one from Nature for another of Dias's papers on room-temperature superconductivity. Such retractions can have significant implications for the researcher's reputation and career.Retractions in academic publishing occur when research findings are proven to be false or misleading due to reasons like data manipulation, plagiarism, or unethical research practices. In this case, it seems an investigation has found compelling evidence of data fabrication or falsification in Dias's work.This is a crucial reminder of the importance of the scientific process, including peer review and ongoing scrutiny even after publication, to ensure the integrity and reliability of published research. It highlights the necessity for caution when interpreting early findings, especially in high-stakes areas such as room-temperature superconductivity.It's worth noting that while this news is a setback for the field, it doesn't mean progress toward room-temperature superconductivity is halted. It makes use of the waste heat generated by computers and other electronics, turning a problem (excess heat) into a solution (home heating).Here's how it could work, in theory:In colder climates, houses and buildings often require heating for a significant part of the year. Computers, servers, and data centers, on the other hand, generate a lot of heat when they're operating. This heat is usually treated as waste and is often actively cooled to prevent overheating, using even more energy in the process.By moving these computers and servers into homes and buildings in colder climates, you could repurpose the waste heat as a heat source. Instead of needing to burn fuel or use electricity to heat the building, you could use the waste heat from the computing operations. This could potentially reduce the carbon footprint and energy costs of heating.However, there are also challenges with this idea:Noise and space: Computers, especially servers, can be quite noisy and take up a lot of space. This could be problematic in a home environment.Maintenance and security: Computers and servers require regular maintenance and are often kept in secure facilities to prevent unauthorized access. This could be more challenging in a home environment.Internet infrastructure: High-performance computing often requires strong and reliable internet connections, which might not be available in all residential areas.Energy efficiency of heating: Computers aren't designed to be efficient heaters. So even though they generate heat, they might not be as efficient or effective at heating a home as a dedicated heating system.Variable heat production: The heat produced by a computer can vary depending on usage, which could lead to inconsistent heating.Environmental impact: The production and disposal of computers and electronic components can have significant environmental impacts. It would be important to consider these lifecycle impacts as well.Despite these challenges, this idea has actually been explored in practice! For example, the company Qarnot Computing has developed a heater that uses high-performance computing to generate heat. And in some places, excess heat from data centers is used to heat nearby homes and buildings. This can be used to push down slowly on a gravitational charger when needed, the blocks have a maze of tunnels through them so when the milkflour truck comes by to fill you milkflour reservoirs, they also use a crane to lift the weight higher, ratcheting the battery and run hot steam through the heat sink to "charge it up" again. This type of system could conceivably be expanded and made more efficient.Heating Giant Blocks of Metal: This idea essentially creates a form of thermal battery. In general, thermal storage involves the use of a medium (like a block of metal) that can absorb and store heat energy, which can then be retrieved later by reversing the process. For instance, hot steam could be run through the block to heat it up, then later that heat could be used for home heating or cooking, as you suggested.Gravitational Charger: Along with the thermal storage, you're also proposing a form of mechanical energy storage, where the potential energy of a lifted object (the heavy metal block) is converted into electrical energy as the object descends. This is a similar principle to pumped-storage hydroelectricity, but on a much smaller, household scale.Delivery of Services: Integrating this system with a regular delivery service is an interesting way to centralize and reduce the energy costs of maintenance. This could be seen as a modern take on the old "milkman" model, where goods and services are delivered directly to consumers' homes on a regular schedule.Overall, the system you're proposing would be quite complex to implement, and there would be a number of technical challenges to overcome. For example, the efficiency of energy transfer and storage in the thermal battery and gravitational charger would need to be high enough to be practical and cost-effective. There would also be safety considerations with handling and storing high-temperature materials and heavy weights in residential areas.Nevertheless, your idea is an imaginative example of how we can think differently about our energy systems and leverage multiple forms of energy transfer and storage to improve efficiency and sustainability.Yeah it all sounds a bit complicated. It might involve new household appliances for processing food and materials as well as for tumbling, storage, and packaging. For example, in our bodies, the bloodstream is a unified transportation network that delivers nutrients and oxygen to cells while also carrying away waste products. Similarly, the lymphatic system works to transport white blood cells and remove cellular waste.Creating a similar unified transportation network in our households or communities, where food and materials are delivered and waste is removed using the same infrastructure, does resemble these biological processes. It's a holistic approach to resource distribution and waste management, and it's an intriguing idea.However, the technical challenges would be quite significant. Developing new appliances for processing and packaging, and creating the infrastructure to support this "gumball economy," would require substantial resources and technological advancements. Itâs official: LK-99 isnât a room-temperature superconductor
Tim De Chant
@tdechant / 10:00 AM ADTâ¢August 18, 2023
Red circle with slash over a levitating superconductor
Image Credits: Tim De Chant / TechCrunch and ktsimage / Getty Images
If there was any hope remaining that LK-99 might be a room-temperature superconductor, itâs pretty much dead now.

In fact, itâs worse than that. Pure samples of the substance show that it is an insulator â the opposite of a superconductor. The glimmers of hope that kept the story in the news for weeks appear to be the result of impurities in the original samples.

Dozens of studies published in the last week or two have coalesced around the conclusion, less than a month after a sensational preprint paper was published by a team at the Quantum Energy Research Centre, a small company housed in the basement of a modest apartment building in Seoul, South Korea.

The Korean team made waves when it published preprints on July 22, claiming to have created a material that exhibited superconductor-like properties at ambient temperature and pressure. Whatâs more, the material was made of plebeian ingredients: lead, copper, phosphorus and oxygen. It flew in the face of decades of research into superconductors.

And yet it appeared to possess some of the same qualities that define superconductors. A video released by the team showed it partially levitating above a magnet, and when probing it for electrical resistance, they noticed a sharp drop around 104.8Â°C. Both the levitation and the resistance drop are hallmarks of superconductors.

Early warnings
There were warnings early on that the claims might be bunk. For one, the team published to a preprint server first. This isnât necessarily a red flag, though preprints are far from the gold standard. Thereâs no peer review of preprints, and the bar for submission is pretty low. In most cases, thatâs not a problem; preprints have allowed many fields to move more quickly than the traditional peer-review process allows, and most scientists arenât making outlandish claims in their preprints. Still, the fact that LK-99 appeared first in a preprint wasnât promising.
